data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>Want to take your <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/10-ways-to-take-stunning-portraits/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"portraits (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">portraits</a> to the next level with lighting ratios?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>You&#8217;ve come to the right place. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Because in this article, I&#8217;m going to share everything you need to know about lighting ratios for <em>amazing </em>portraits, including:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>What lighting ratios are</li><li>Basic ratios for portrait photography</li><li>Tips for using ratios</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>(And much more!)</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So if you want to become a portrait photography master, then let&#8217;s get started.</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/lighting-ratios.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"lighting ratios portrait photography\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"what-is-a-lighting-ratio-in-photography\">What is a lighting ratio in photography?</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>As a math term, a ratio is a <em>comparison</em> of one thing to another.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So when calculating <em>lighting </em>ratios, you measure the light falling on the light or highlight side of the face and compare it to the light falling on the shadow side of the face. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>For instance, if you have twice as much light falling on the highlight side of your portrait, then the lighting ratio would be 2:1. If you have four times as much light falling on the highlight side of your portrait, then the lighting ratio would be 4:1.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>But how exactly do you measure light? </p>\n\n\n\n<p>While you can do it with the <a aria-label=\"built-in meter in your camera (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/metering-modes-and-how-your-camera-meter-works/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">built-in meter in your camera</a>, it is much easier and more accurate to use a <a aria-label=\"handheld incident light meter (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/tips-for-using-a-handheld-light-meter/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">handheld incident light meter</a>. You see, your in-camera meter takes a measurement of the light reflecting off your subject &#8211; whereas a handheld meter can measure the amount of light falling <em>on </em>your subject and therefore gives you a more accurate value.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"lighting-ratios-key-concepts\">Lighting ratios: key concepts</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>To properly measure and understand lighting ratios, you need to know a few things. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>First, light is measured by <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/aperture/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"f-stops (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">f-stops</a>. The aperture dial on your camera generally goes up in 1/3-stop increments, though the full stops for aperture are f/1, f/1.4, f/2, f/2.8, f/4, f/5.6, f/8, f/11, f/16, f/22, f/32, etc. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>(A simple way to remember all the numbers is to memorize f/1 and f/1.4; all the other pairs are doubled from these with a few rounded off.)</p>\n\n\n\n<p>You also need to understand that <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/shutter-speed/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"shutter speeds (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">shutter speeds</a> are also representative of f-stops, with the full stops at 1s, Â½s, Â¼s, 1/8s, 1/15s, 1/30s, 1/60s, 1/125s, 1/250s, 1/500s, 1/1000s, etc. Shutter speed full stops are easier to remember as they are generally doubled (with a couple instances of rounding, such as 1/8s to 1/15s).</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Finally, know that each full stop is <em>double </em>(or <em>half</em>, depending on whether you go down or up) the amount of light compared to the previous one. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>For example, if you are shooting at f/4 and want to shoot at f/5.6, you will need to double the amount of light to get an equivalent exposure (you&#8217;ll need one more full stop of light). If you want to narrow your aperture by 2 stops, you&#8217;ll need to add 2&#215;2 &#8211; or 4 &#8211; times more light. Likewise, 3 stops correspond to 8 times more light (2x2x2) and so on.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Knowing this, you can figure out how to create and measure ratios. In the next series of photos, I will demonstrate four different lighting ratios and how they were achieved.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"ratio\">1:1 ratio</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/1-1ratio-darlenehildebrandt.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"1:1 lighting ratio\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>A 1:1 ratio is even lighting.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In other words, there is no difference in the meter readings on both sides of the face. As you can see in the photo above, 1:1 lighting is very flat, and it can be achieved in a couple of different ways. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>First, you can use <a aria-label=\"fill flash (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/using-fill-flash/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">fill flash</a> and make the flash equal to the main light source. This is harder to achieve until youâve had some practice, and youâll often end up overpowering the light with flash. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Secondly, you could use a <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/10-ways-use-5-1-reflector/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"reflector (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">reflector</a>. It will need to be <em>very </em>close to the subject, and the goal is to eliminate all shadows on the subject&#8217;s face.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>This is a 1:1 ratio, and it&#8217;s pretty easy to see and recognize visually.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"ratio\">2:1 ratio</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/1-2ratio-darlenehildebrandt.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"2:1 lighting ratio portrait photography\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>As the numbers suggest, a 2:1 ratio occurs when one side has twice as much light compared to the other.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So knowing that plus what you know about f-stops, we can set up this lighting ratio.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>First, put your subject into the light where you are going to photograph them. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Using a light meter, measure the light falling on the side of their face closest to the light source (i.e., the highlight side). Letâs say that measures f/8. (Keep your shutter speed the same for all measurements to maintain consistency.) </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Then bring in your reflector and use the light meter again, but this time measure the light falling on the side of the subject&#8217;s face that is <em>farther away</em> from the light source (from here on in, this will be referred to as the <em>shadow side</em>).</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Note: If you are using a handheld meter, make sure to shield it from excess light that comes from the other side of your subject. So if you are metering the shadow side nearer the reflector, shield the meter so the main light source isn&#8217;t hitting it.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Now, you <em>know </em>that your highlight side has a reading of f/8. To achieve a 2:1 lighting ratio, you&#8217;ll need to get your meter to read f/5.6 (which is one stop less light) on the shadow side. So adjust the reflector distance until the meter settles on f/5.6.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Also, it may help to study the image above and recognize the contrast range from the highlight to the shadow side of the face. It&#8217;s subtle, but you should be able to see it.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"ratio\">4:1 ratio</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/4-1ratio-darlenehildebrandt.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"4:1 lighting ratio portrait photography\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>A 4:1 ratio is double a 2:1 ratio. So if 2:1 featured twice as much light &#8211; or one stop &#8211; how much light will a 4:1 ratio require? </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The answer is <em>four </em>times as much light (2&#215;2), which is a 2-stop difference from the highlight side to the shadow side of the face.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>I teach an available light class, and I always recommend working with natural light before you advance on to <a aria-label=\"speedlights (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/take-your-light-with-you-the-power-of-speedlights/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">speedlights</a>. With available or natural light and a reflector, it is <em>much</em> easier to learn and practice lighting because you can <em>see </em>what happens as you make changes (WYSIWYG). Flash is harder to predict as you canât see it without actually taking a photograph.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So if our main light (the window) is still at f/8, what should be the value of our fill light/reflector (or the shadow side) to achieve a 4:1 ratio? Letâs do the steps again: f/8>f/5.6>f/4. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Therefore, two stops less than f/8 is f/4 (and this is the desired measurement to create a 4:1 ratio). Look at the photo above and compare it to the 2:1 image; do you see how the shadow side is getting darker?</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"ratio\">8:1 ratio</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/8-1ratio-darlenehildebrandt.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"8:1 lighting ratio\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>The last ratio weâll look at is 8:1. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The 8:1 ratio requires 8 times as much light, or 3 stops, on the highlight side of the face compared to the shadow side.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>As you can see in the image above, 8:1 lighting is quite dramatic, and anything greater than 8:1 will not hold much detail on the shadow side of the face at all. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>In fact, prints have a maximum contrast range of 4-6 stops, so unless you want one side of the face overexposed or the shadows pure black, I suggest keeping your lighting ratios at 8:1 or smaller.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Note that an 8:1 ratio can be a bit tough to create. You may need harsher lighting and possibly a black reflector to add blacks into the shadow side (rather than reflecting light onto it). </p>\n\n\n\n<p>We calculate it the same way as above: if 4:1 is 4 times the light, then 8:1 will be 8 times the light, or 3 stops. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So if we are still at f/8 on our highlight side, we need to get our shadow side to read: f/8>f/5.6>f/4>f/2.8. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"how-to-use-lighting-ratios\">How to use lighting ratios</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Now that we have this knowledge of ratios, let&#8217;s put it to use! Remember: The ratio can add to the success of your portrait, or it can ruin it. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>If you look at the example images again, pay attention to how the mood of the image changes with the ratio. Notice how the higher ratios create more drama and power. Notice how the lower ratios are softer and more innocent.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Generally somewhere between 2:1 and 4:1 is the commonly used ratio for most portraiture. Itâs enough to create three-dimensionality on the face, but not too much to create unattractive, deep shadows. I personally like a 3:1 ratio (1.5 stops) or a 4:1 ratio, myself.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>For a child or baby, you often want a lower ratio because the softer result goes well with the subject matter. But a grizzled old cowboy with weathered, wrinkled skin and unkempt whiskers looks much better with 4:1 or 8:1 lighting. Why? He&#8217;s rougher, tougher, and can handle the increased contrast, plus it&#8217;s suitable for his look.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>If you&#8217;re worried about not owning a handheld meter, you don&#8217;t necessarily need to go out and buy one. I&#8217;d only suggest you do that if you plan on getting into studio lighting. Otherwise, just practice seeing the difference between the various ratios (which is why I suggest natural light), and if they aren&#8217;t a perfect 4:1 or 8:1 or whatever, then it doesn&#8217;t matter. Just learn to recognize when it&#8217;s too strong and when it&#8217;s too weak for the effect you want to create.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Here&#8217;s another example of ratios at work. None of it is right or wrong, but which do you think is the most appropriate ratio for my subject? Figure that part out, and you&#8217;ll be ahead of the game!</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image caption-align-left is-style-with-caption\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/strong-ratio.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"headshot without a reflector\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/><figcaption>My first shot had no reflector, and I found the shadows too dark and the overall portrait too contrasty. So I brought in a reflector for the next shot.</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image caption-align-left is-style-with-caption\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/weak-ratio.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"headshot with a reflector\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/><figcaption>This is the second image I took, this time with a silver reflector. I found the ratio to be almost 1:1, and I wanted a bit more drama for a man&#8217;s portrait.</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image caption-align-left is-style-with-caption\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/good-ratio.jpg?ssl=1\" alt=\"headshot with a white reflector\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/><figcaption>For this last image, I switched to a white reflector and backed it off a bit to get a ratio that I was happy with.</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p><em>Note: I&#8217;d like to thank my subject, <a href=\"http://ruinism.com/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Gabriel Biderman</a> from B&#38;H Photo Video, for these shots.</em></p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"lighting-ratio-tips\">Lighting ratio tips</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Now that you understand the lighting ratio basics, let&#8217;s take a look at some quick tips that&#8217;ll make your photos look better.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>First, <em>get your subject out of the sun</em>. Shooting in bright sunlight makes it almost impossible to control your ratios, plus the light won&#8217;t be desirable or flattering on the subject&#8217;s face. Use <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/are-you-practicing-these-5-natural-lighting-tips/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"natural light (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">natural light</a> from a window with indirect lighting (no direct sun coming in) if you can. And if the sun is streaming in, try adding sheer curtains or even stretching a white bed sheet across to diffuse the light.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Second, here are some lighting ratio starting points:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>For babies and small children, use a lower ratio like 1:1 or 2:1. Children move so quickly that keeping them in the light and facing in the right direction is exceedingly difficult, and even light helps mitigate this issue.</li><li>For women, use a medium ratio like 2:1 or 3:1.</li><li>For men and business portraits, use a slightly stronger ratio, such as 4:1 or 6:1.</li><li>For artists, bands, and other dramatic portraits, use a higher ratio.</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>Finally, keep in mind that there are no steadfast rules on how to do ratios in portraiture. Like everything in photography, it&#8217;s about learning the techniques, then using them as suggestions while you experiment and find your own style or voice. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>For instance, can you put 8:1 lighting on a glamorous movie star or 1:1 lighting on a coal miner? Absolutely! </p>\n\n\n\n<p>(I&#8217;ll even give you some homework that&#8217;ll prove both ratios can be effective if they are done well.)</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"homework-and-action-steps\">Homework and action steps</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Your homework assignment is to research the following photographers. Tell me how they broke the general rules I&#8217;ve mentioned above, yet still had great success and amazing images:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li><a aria-label=\"George Hurrell (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://georgehurrell.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">George Hurrell</a></li><li><a aria-label=\"Richard Avedon (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://www.avedonfoundation.org/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Richard Avedon</a></li><li><a href=\"https://karsh.org/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"Yousuf Karsh (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Yousuf Karsh</a></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>Those three photographers right there are some of the greatest portrait artists to ever live. Learn from them.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"a-lighting-ratios-guide-conclusion\">A lighting ratios guide: conclusion</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Now that you&#8217;ve finished this article, you know all about lighting ratios &#8211; and you know how to use them for stunning results. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So what&#8217;s the next step?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Practice! If you simply dedicate a few minutes per day to working with portrait lighting, you&#8217;ll become a master in no time at all.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Now over to you:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>What do you think of these lighting ratio concepts? Will they help you in your portrait photography? Share your thoughts (and images) in the comments below!</em></p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/lighting-ratios-to-make-or-break-your-portrait/\">A Lighting Ratios Guide: How to Make (or Break) Your Portraits</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com\">Digital Photography School</a>. You&#8217;ve come to the right place.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In this article, I&#8217;m going to share my 17 favorite editing apps &#8211; including apps for general editing, apps for fun and filters, and apps for specialized editing. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So whether you&#8217;re a dedicated <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/tips-better-smartphone-photography/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"smartphone photographer (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">smartphone photographer</a> or just looking to find some powerful ways to edit on the go, this list contains the perfect app for your needs.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Let&#8217;s get started. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"the-best-photo-editing-apps-for-general-editing\">The best photo-editing apps for general editing</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>In this section, you&#8217;ll discover the <strong>best photo-editing apps for general adjustments</strong>. With the apps on this list, you can adjust exposure, enhance colors, crop, sharpen, and more.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"snapseed\">1. Snapseed</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-202449\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; padding-top:46.13%\"></span><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1500\" height=\"692\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Snapseed.jpg?resize=1500%2C692&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"best photo editing apps snapseed\" class=\"wp-image-202449\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Snapseed.jpg?w=1500&#38;ssl=1 1500w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Snapseed.jpg?resize=300%2C138&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Snapseed.jpg?resize=600%2C277&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Snapseed.jpg?resize=768%2C354&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Snapseed.jpg?resize=717%2C331&#38;ssl=1 717w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<ul><li><strong>Cost:</strong> Free </li><li><strong>Availability:</strong> <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/snapseed/id439438619\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"iOS (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">iOS</a> and <a href=\"https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.niksoftware.snapseed&#38;hl=en_US&#38;gl=US\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"Android (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Android</a></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>Snapseed is one of the most popular photo-editing apps on the market &#8211; and for good reason. It&#8217;s very intuitive and easy to use, which makes it great for beginners. At the same time, Snapseed offers a lot of control for more skilled users.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>You don&#8217;t need to pay any fees for using Snapseed, nor are there in-app upgrades; it&#8217;s completely free. So if you&#8217;re after a beginner-friendly photo editor that can do pretty much anything, Snapseed is a great choice.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"lightroom\">2. Lightroom </h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-202439\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; padding-top:46.13%\"></span><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1500\" height=\"692\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Lightroom.jpg?resize=1500%2C692&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"best photo editing apps lightroom\" class=\"wp-image-202439\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Lightroom.jpg?w=1500&#38;ssl=1 1500w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Lightroom.jpg?resize=300%2C138&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Lightroom.jpg?resize=600%2C277&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Lightroom.jpg?resize=768%2C354&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Lightroom.jpg?resize=717%2C331&#38;ssl=1 717w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<ul><li><strong>Cost:</strong> Limited version for free. Full version available with an Adobe subscription (from $9.99).</li><li><strong>Availability:</strong> <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/adobe-lightroom-photo-editor/id878783582\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"iOS (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">iOS</a> and <a href=\"https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.adobe.lrmobile&#38;hl=en_US&#38;gl=US\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"Android (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Android</a></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>The free version of <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/setting-lightroom-mobile-first-time-users/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"Lightroom Mobile (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">Lightroom Mobile</a> lets you do most basic editing tasks. You can also access and create presets. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>However, if you decide to upgrade, you&#8217;ll gain access to a healing brush, selective adjustments, geometry tools, and RAW editing. You can also use the camera from the app to shoot RAW images. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>If you want to use Lightroom Mobile to speed up your workflow, check out <a aria-label=\"this article (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/how-to-use-lightroom-mobile-to-speed-up-your-workflow/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">this article</a>.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"photoshop-express\">3. Its mobile version is divided into three smaller and specialized apps; the basic editor is <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/photoshop-express/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"Photoshop Express (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">Photoshop Express</a>.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Photoshop Express offers one-touch solutions such as an auto-fix option and filters. It also allows you to edit and retouch with total control. And it&#8217;s packed with many fun features such as stickers and collages. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>You can crop to most platform&#8217;s formats and share directly from the app. While you don&#8217;t need an Adobe subscription to use Photoshop Express, you will need a free account.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"pixlr\">4. effects. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>(In most cases, these apps also include a camera and some basic adjustments tools.)</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"vsco\">5. social-media-focused features.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>To use VSCO, you will need to create a (free) account. After that, you can continue to use VSCO for free, but most of the filters are sold separately. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Alternatively, you can grab a yearly subscription for $19.99 that includes 200 filters (and you have a 7-day trial to make sure it&#8217;s worth it).</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Your creations can then be shared with the community, which is like- and comments-free. In other words, the VSCO community is more about the quality of the work. You can also share VSCO creations directly from the app to other social networks such as Instagram or Snapchat.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"prisma-photo-editor\">6. Full version is $29.99/year.</li><li><strong>Availability:</strong> <a aria-label=\"iOS (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/prisma-photo-editor/id1122649984\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">iOS</a> and <a aria-label=\"Android (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.neuralprisma&#38;hl=en_US&#38;gl=US\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Android</a></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>Prisma is designed for art lovers. It&#8217;s more than just filters; Prisma uses artificial intelligence to turn your photos into artworks inspired by the greatest artists in history. And unlike other apps, Prisma adds new filters every day. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>General editing is also possible (as it is with most filter apps). Though I do miss the rotation tool to correct the horizon if needed. </p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"picsart-photo-editor\">7. You can do anything with PicsArt, from professional applications like time-lapse photography to fun stickers and drawing. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>PicsArt Photo Editor also has social media integration, as well as thematic contests that will spark your creativity.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"a-color-story\">8. A Color Story</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-202435\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; You can do some basic editing and apply filters to your images and videos. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>New filter collections based on current trends are added often, although most do need to be purchased separately. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>You can even plan your Instagram feed with the Grid feature. In fact, you can use this just to see how the feed looks after each picture you add, or you can connect it to your Instagram account for scheduling posts. That&#8217;s why A Color Story is great for maintaining a <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/how-to-make-a-unified-instagram-feed-as-a-photographer/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">unified Instagram feed</a> &#8211; whether you are a photographer, influencer, or community manager. </p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"afterlight-photo-editor\">9. Some of these tools are offered for a small fee, but there is also a wide variety of free, high-quality effects to choose from.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Afterlight can also handle basic editing tasks; the app has 15 tools to make most of the adjustments you&#8217;ll need. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The Crop tool is also quite versatile; it allows you to straighten, flip, and crop freehand and to many standard ratios.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"photo-lab-picture-editor\">10. you can have fun or do some professional-looking work. You can even turn your photos into cartoons, and you can swap faces and make collages.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Photo Lab is an app designed to give free rein to your creativity. It&#8217;s also a social app with a big community of followers that&#8217;ll help you stay inspired. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"best-apps-for-specialized-editing\">Best apps for specialized editing</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>If you&#8217;re looking for the best photo-editing apps with dedicated features, this is the list for you:</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"foodie\">11. Foodie&#8217;s in-app camera to take pictures (and you&#8217;ll get a live view of your selected filter). There are plenty of food filter series, including Fresh, BBQ, Yum, and more. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Once you&#8217;ve chosen a filter, you can compose your image using the smart grid. For flat lays, you&#8217;ll get a yellow band across the edges of the screen when the camera is perfectly level. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Foodie is only missing one key editing feature, but it&#8217;s a big one: a crop tool. You get the main tools for basic retouching such as exposure, contrast, etc. And Photoshop Fix also offers a separate Light tool, which allows you to selectively adjust highlights and shadows.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>But the real power is in the Liquify tool. With it, you can smooth the skin and even adjust expressions to make your subjects smile.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Photoshop Fix is free, but you will need to create an Adobe account.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"skrwt\">13. And if you&#8217;re removing a line (e.g., telephone wires in the background), you just need to tap; it will automatically be selected and removed.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>TouchRetouch also has a clone tool, plus a feature called Quick Fix to remove blemishes. Many apps offer some kind of healing brush, but as a specialized app, TouchRetouch will get you the best results.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"photoshop-mix\">15. You can also work with <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/add-texture-overlay-images-stunning-effect/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"texture overlays (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">texture overlays</a> thanks to Photoshop Mix&#8217;s different <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/video-tutorial-comprehensive-photoshop-blend-modes/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">blending modes</a>. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>As with the other Adobe apps on this list, you&#8217;ll need to create a (free) account to use Photoshop Mix, but you don&#8217;t need a subscription.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"motionleap-formerly-pixaloop\">16. Motionleap (formerly Pixaloop)</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-202445\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; padding-top:46.53%\"></span><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1500\" height=\"698\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Pixaloop.jpg?resize=1500%2C698&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"pixaloop\" class=\"wp-image-202445\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Pixaloop.jpg?w=1500&#38;ssl=1 1500w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Pixaloop.jpg?resize=300%2C140&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Pixaloop.jpg?resize=600%2C279&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Pixaloop.jpg?resize=768%2C357&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-Pixaloop.jpg?resize=717%2C334&#38;ssl=1 717w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<ul id=\"block-0cc0f2a9-d390-4c49-aacc-39a565f7623b\"><li><strong>Cost:</strong> Free limited version, or one of three paid choices: $3.50/month, $18/year, or a one-time purchase of $55.</li><li><strong>Availability:</strong> <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/motionleap-by-lightricks/id1381206010\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"iOS (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">iOS</a> and <a href=\"https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.lightricks.pixaloop&#38;hl=en_US&#38;gl=US\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"Android (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Android</a></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>Have you seen pictures where everything is motionless &#8211; except for the water running or the coffee steam coming out of a cup? Well, Motionleap lets you create that effect with just a few taps and swipes.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>You can also add filters, do some basic adjustments, and apply overlays. Keep in mind that the free version won&#8217;t let you export your projects and you won&#8217;t have all the tools available, so it&#8217;s worth considering a paid subscription.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3 data-section id=\"canva\">17. Canva</h3>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-202437\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; padding-top:66.67%\"></span><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1500\" height=\"1000\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-canva.jpg?resize=1500%2C1000&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"best photo editing apps canva\" class=\"wp-image-202437\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-canva.jpg?w=1500&#38;ssl=1 1500w, https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-canva.jpg?resize=300%2C200&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-canva.jpg?resize=600%2C400&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-canva.jpg?resize=768%2C512&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-canva.jpg?resize=90%2C60&#38;ssl=1 90w, https://i2.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Best-photoediting-apps-canva.jpg?resize=717%2C478&#38;ssl=1 717w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<ul id=\"block-346e2a0c-1469-45d9-8b2c-899d9734423a\"><li><strong>Cost:</strong> Free</li><li><strong>Availability:</strong> <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/canva-graphic-design-video/id897446215\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"iOS (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">iOS</a> and <a href=\"https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.canva.editor&#38;hl=en_US&#38;gl=US\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"Android (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Android</a></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>If you use your photography for marketing purposes, then Canva is the app for you. It offers enough graphic design templates to fit your every need. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>From creating an eye-catching Facebook post to designing an entire menu, Canva is intuitive and easy to use. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Many templates are free, though others must be purchased separately. Canva also has a browser version so you can access your projects on your computer, too. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"best-photo-editing-apps-final-words\">Best photo-editing apps: final words</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Well, that&#8217;s it:</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The best photo-editing apps available in 2021! So start downloading your favorites. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And remember that you don&#8217;t need to pick just one; you can use two apps, three apps, or more to improve your editing workflow.</p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>Do you like any other photo-editing</em> <em>apps? Are there apps that should be added to this list? Share your thoughts in the comments below!</em></p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"faq\">FAQ</h2>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"schema-faq wp-block-yoast-faq-block\"><div class=\"schema-faq-section\" id=\"faq-question-1610451651731\"><strong class=\"schema-faq-question\">Are paid photo-editing apps better than free photo-editing apps?</strong> <p class=\"schema-faq-answer\">No, there are excellent free choices such as Snapseed and Photoshop Express. </p> </div> <div class=\"schema-faq-section\" id=\"faq-question-1610451681683\"><strong class=\"schema-faq-question\">Should I have more than one editing app?</strong> <p class=\"schema-faq-answer\">That depends on your needs. In my experience, it is useful to have different apps for different tasks.</p> </div> <div class=\"schema-faq-section\" id=\"faq-question-1610452458765\"><strong class=\"schema-faq-question\">What if I like smartphone photography but prefer to edit on my computer?<br /></strong> <p class=\"schema-faq-answer\">If you don&#8217;t like editing on your phone, you need to use an app that allows you to easily transfer pictures between your phone and your computer. You can use Lightroom for this (with an Adobe subscription). Another choice is to use a cloud service such as Dropbox. </p> </div> <div class=\"schema-faq-section\" id=\"faq-question-1610452193569\"><strong class=\"schema-faq-question\">What is the best photo-editing app?</strong> <p class=\"schema-faq-answer\">I don&#8217;t think there&#8217;s one app that tops all the others; I think it&#8217;s about which one is best for you. That will depend on your phone, your budget, and your editing needs &#8211; plus your personal preference. </p> </div> </div>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/best-photo-editing-apps/\">The 17 Best Photo-Editing Apps (in 2021)</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com\">Digital Photography School</a>. And how do these sensor differences affect your photos? </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Thinking about camera sensors can get pretty confusing. That&#8217;s why I break it all down for you in this article &#8211; and I offer plenty of examples to illustrate the camera sensor effects.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So if you want to know, once and for all, how sensor type impacts your images, then let&#8217;s get started.</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-157587\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; padding-top:47.4%\"></span><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1500\" height=\"711\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_4.jpg?resize=1500%2C711&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Full Frame VS Crop Sensor VS Micro Four Thirds: Camera Sensors Explained\" class=\"wp-image-157587\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_4.jpg?w=1500&#38;ssl=1 1500w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_4.jpg?resize=300%2C142&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_4.jpg?resize=768%2C364&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_4.jpg?resize=600%2C284&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_4.jpg?resize=717%2C340&#38;ssl=1 717w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"what-is-a-full-frame-vs-an-aps-c-crop-vs-a-micro-four-thirds-mft-camera\">What is a full-frame vs an APS-C (crop) vs a Micro Four Thirds (MFT) camera? </h2>\n\n\n\n<p>The sensor is the part of a camera that actually captures an image. It takes in light, which it then converts to image data. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Now, different camera types offer different-sized sensors, and that&#8217;s the fundamental difference between full-frame, APS-C, and Micro Four Thirds cameras.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>A <a aria-label=\"full-frame camera (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/understand-differences-full-frame-versus-crop-sensor-cameras/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">full-frame camera</a> contains a sensor size equivalent to 35mm film (36 mm x 24 mm). This is the largest sensor size marketed to photography consumers.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>An APS-C camera, on the other hand, has a smaller sensor. The specifics depend on the camera brand, but the sensor size is generally around 23 mm x 15 mm.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Finally, there are <a aria-label=\"Micro Four Thirds cameras (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/are-micro-four-thirds-cameras-dead-or-do-they-still-have-their-place/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">Micro Four Thirds cameras</a>, which contain Micro Four Thirds sensors; these are even smaller than APS-C sensors, clocking in at just 17.3 mm x 13 mm.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Now, apart from the physical sizes, there are several important differences between full-frame, APS-C, and Micro Four Thirds sensors.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So letâs take a look at the factors affected by sensor size, starting with:</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"crop-factor\">Crop factor</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Say you mount a 50mm lens on a full-frame camera. When you press the shutter button, it captures a 50mm image. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Makes sense, right? A 50mm lens captures a 50mm image. Simple.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>But what if you mount a 50mm lens on an APS-C camera? Will it capture a 50mm image? </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The answer is <em>no</em>.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Since an APS-C sensor is <em>smaller </em>than the full-frame sensor, the sensor <em>crops </em>the frame, giving you a result that looks zoomed in &#8211; as if you took the photo with a 75mm lens rather than a 50mm lens.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>(Confused? Don&#8217;t be. The effect is similar to taking a photo with a 50mm lens, then heading home and cropping the image on your computer. You&#8217;ll get a tighter shot, one that looks like it was taken with a longer lens.)</p>\n\n\n\n<p>And that is what the term <em><a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/crop-factor-explained/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">crop factor</a> </em>means. It refers to the different crop effects created by different sensor sizes. A full-frame camera is the standard; it has no crop factor. An APS-C sensor (also known as a crop sensor), has a crop factor of 1.5x (on Nikon and Sony cameras) or 1.6x (on Canon cameras). The Micro Four Thirds crop factor is even stronger: 2x.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>As I explained above, the crop factor affects your field of view. Look at the series of images below:</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image caption-align-left wp-image-157577 size-full is-style-with-caption\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-157577\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; padding-top:23.27%\"></span><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1500\" height=\"349\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_1.jpg?resize=1500%2C349&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"full frame vs crop vs Micro Four Thirds sensors compared\" class=\"wp-image-157577\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_1.jpg?w=1500&#38;ssl=1 1500w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_1.jpg?resize=300%2C70&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_1.jpg?resize=768%2C179&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_1.jpg?resize=600%2C140&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_1.jpg?resize=717%2C167&#38;ssl=1 717w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span><figcaption>Left: Photo taken with a full-frame camera. Center: Photo taken with a crop-sensor camera. Right: Photo taken with a Micro Four Thirds camera.</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>The image on the left was taken with a full-frame camera. But apply the APS-C crop factor, and you get a tighter shot (the center image). Use a Micro Four Thirds camera, and you&#8217;ll get an even tighter result (the right image).</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"focal-length\">Focal length</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>A crop factor has a predictable affect on your lens&#8217;s focal length. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>You see, the focal length measurement of any given lens is based on the standard 35mm film format. And since an APS-C camera (and a Micro Four Thirds camera) crop out the edges of the frame, you end up with an &#8220;effective&#8221; focal length that corresponds directly to the original focal length <em>multiplied </em>by the crop factor.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>For example, a crop-sensor camera such as the <a aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://geni.us/NikonD5600dPS\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">Nikon D5600</a> has a crop factor of 1.5x. Thus, if I mount a 35mm lens on my Nikon D5600, it would multiply the focal length by 1.5x, effectively giving me a focal length output of around 52.5mm. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>(But if you mount the same lens on a full-frame Nikon body such as the <a href=\"https://geni.us/dPSNikonD850Camera\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">D850</a>, it gives an output of 35mm.)</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Similarly, if you mount a 35mm lens on a Micro Four Thirds camera &#8211; which has a crop factor of 2x &#8211; it effectively <em>doubles </em>the focal length to around 70mm.</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image wp-image-157582 size-full is-style-with-caption\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-157582\" style=\"width:1500px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1500px; padding-top:23.27%\"></span><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1500\" height=\"349\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_2.jpg?resize=1500%2C349&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Three images taken with different sensors\" class=\"wp-image-157582\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_2.jpg?w=1500&#38;ssl=1 1500w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_2.jpg?resize=300%2C70&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_2.jpg?resize=768%2C179&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_2.jpg?resize=600%2C140&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/digital-photography-school.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/full_frame_vs_crop_sensor_vs_micro_four_thirds_2.jpg?resize=717%2C167&#38;ssl=1 717w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></span><figcaption>Left: Photo taken at 35mm on a full-frame camera. Center: Photo taken at 35mm on a crop-sensor camera. Right: Photo taken at 35mm on a Micro Four Thirds camera.</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"depth-of-field\">Depth of field</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>As with focal length, a multiplier effect gets applied to the <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/aperture/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"aperture (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">aperture</a> when using APS-C and MFT cameras. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The aperture or f-stop is one of several factors determining the <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/understanding-depth-field-beginners/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">depth of field</a>. Thus, a Micro Four Thirds camera gives us more depth of field when compared to a full-frame camera, assuming both cameras are using equivalent effective focal lengths. Same with an APS-C camera compared to a full-frame camera; you get more depth of field using the APS-C camera, assuming the effective focal length on both cameras is identical.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>For example, an image shot at f/1.8 on a Micro Four Thirds camera gives an output similar to an image shot at f/3.6 on a full-frame camera and f/2.7 on a crop-sensor camera. This is assuming that the effective focal length and other shooting conditions stay the same.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"camera-size\">Camera size</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Full-frame sensors are larger than APS-C and Micro Four Thirds sensors. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So as you can probably guess, full-frame cameras tend to be far bigger and heavier than their APS-C and MFT counterparts. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>For some photographers, this won&#8217;t matter much; if you shoot in the studio every day, a smaller Micro Four Thirds camera won&#8217;t offer much of an advantage. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>But if you&#8217;re a travel photographer who needs to keep your gear as lightweight and compact as possible, a Micro Four Thirds body is a fantastic option. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Plus, APS-C and MFT cameras are more convenient. You can hang them on your neck or keep them in a backpack all day without feeling like you&#8217;re carrying a brick.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"low-light-performance\">Low-light performance</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Generally, full-frame cameras feature superior low-light and <a aria-label=\"high-ISO performance (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/iso-settings/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">high-ISO performance</a>. This results in much better image quality than crop-sensor (or Micro Four Thirds) cameras can achieve.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>But why do full-frame cameras perform better in low light?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Full-frame cameras&#160;have larger sensors and are therefore capable of capturing more light than their smaller-sensor counterparts, which minimizes <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/how-to-avoid-and-reduce-noise-in-your-images/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"unwanted noise (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">unwanted noise</a>.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>For instance, Micro Four Thirds cameras don&#8217;t perform well under low-light conditions where the ISO needs to be cranked up to, say, above 1600.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>(Note that full-frame cameras <em>also </em>offer superior <a href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/understanding-dynamic-range-photography/\" target=\"_blank\" aria-label=\"dynamic range (opens in a new tab)\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">dynamic range</a>, which allows you to capture more detail in a single shot.)</p>\n\n\n\n<p>For these reasons, while full-frame cameras can be expensive, bulky, and annoying to carry around, they are still the industry standard and the preferred cameras for nearly all professional photography work.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 data-section id=\"full-frame-vs-aps-c-vs-micro-four-thirds-conclusion\">Full frame vs APS-C vs Micro Four Thirds: conclusion</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Now that you&#8217;ve finished this article, you should hopefully have a grasp on the differences between these sensor types &#8211; and why you might want to choose one sensor over another. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Just remember: </p>\n\n\n\n<p>All three sensors &#8211; full frame, APS-C, and MFT &#8211; are <em>very </em>capable of capturing stunning photos. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So don&#8217;t worry <em>too </em>much about the differences.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Now over to you:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>Do you have a favorite sensor type? Which of these three sensors does your camera use? Share your thoughts in the comments below!</em></p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/camera-sensors-explained/\">Full Frame vs APS-C vs Micro Four Thirds: Camera Sensors Explained</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com\">Digital Photography School</a>. itâs literally a <em>pocket-sized </em>full-frame camera, one that you can take anywhere yet boasts the power of a much larger model.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The fp L joins Sigmaâs current full-frame mirrorless offering, the <a aria-label=\"Sigma fp (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://geni.us/SigmafpdPS\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener nofollow\" class=\"ek-link\">Sigma fp</a>, another ultra-compact, ultra-light camera that launched back in 2019.Â </p>\n\n\n\n<p>But while the Sigma fp appealed strongly to videographers, the fp L seems to be more stills focused. First, thereâs the 61-megapixel sensor, which serves up plenty of resolution for landscape shooters as well as commercial photographers in need of detailed files.Â </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Then thereâs a 3.68M-dot external <a aria-label=\"electronic viewfinder (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/optical-versus-electronic-viewfinders-best/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">electronic viewfinder</a>, which attaches to the side of the fp L and allows photographers to work with the camera the way theyâd work with high-resolution competitors such as the <a aria-label=\"Sony a7R IV (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://digital-photography-school.com/review-sony-a7r-iv-mirrorless-camera/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" class=\"ek-link\">Sony a7R IV</a>. As a stills photographer, I canât fathom the idea of shooting <em>without </em>a viewfinder, so this is a welcome addition to the fp lineup.Â </p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-206333\" style=\"width:1125px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:1125px; Of course, zoom in too far and you sacrifice significant megapixels, but given the impressive starting resolution, it may come in handy if you need a bit of extra reach.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The fp L also promises improved autofocus over the fp, thanks to a hybrid autofocus system that combines contrast-detection and phase-detection AF for âsmooth autofocus that is high precision, fast, and excellent at tracking a moving subject.â While the fpâs contrast-detection system was certainly serviceable, street photographers, portrait photographers, and travel photographers will appreciate the boost in speed and tracking, as will plenty of videographers.Â </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Interestingly, Sigma also highlights another new feature of the fp L: âUSB-C charging <em>while the camera is on</em>.â In other words, you can pop in the battery, start shooting, and then &#8211; if the battery gets low &#8211; you can plug the camera into a mobile power bank for what Sigma is calling âunlimited power supply.â If youâre a photographer who spends days in the field but doesnât want to juggle stacks of batteries, this will be a huge help, and videographers will love the ability to film for hours without worry.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Speaking of videography:&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>While the fp L may not be as video-centric as the fp, itâs perfectly capable of pro-level recording; you can shoot 4K/30p footage, and you get plenty of other video features, such as a mic port, zebra patterning, and RAW recording.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Regarding lenses: the fp L is compatible with L-mount glass from Sigma, Leica, and Panasonic, including a host of powerful Sigma L-mount lenses.Â </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So for those looking for a tiny camera that punches far above its weight, the Sigma fp L is a great option. the idea behind these challenges being to make you think about what you&#8217;re photographing and work at getting better every time. </p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-full\"><span class=\"responsive-image wp-image-206869\" style=\"width:2272px\"><span class=\"space\" style=\"width:2272px;
